Oh, my sons, since I have completed this book for you, which is the last of the science of medicine, and it was accomplished by its completion, and through its explanations and declarations, it seems to me that you will be able to grasp this treatise, which is a part of surgery with me.
Since the surgery with me is almost completely unknown in our region and in our time, to the point that great danger threatens its science, and its legacy remains hidden; and it will not remain otherwise than a few small descriptions in the books of the Ancients, which have been altered much and have deviated from those errors and hesitation that are enclosed within their intentions, and the force of the art has been weakened.
And it seems to me that you have brought this art to life with the arrangement of this treatise in that second way of exposition and declaration and abbreviation; and so that you may know the forms of the cauterizing instruments and other tools of the craft as well as for the addition of explanation and preparation of them, which is necessary.
But the reason why no skilled artisan is found with their hands in our time, is because the art of medicine is lengthy, and for its practitioner to exercise before this, it is necessary to be exercised in the science of anatomy which Galen knew thoroughly, as he was familiar with the joints of the members and their forms, and their conjunction and separation; and knowledge of bones, nerves, and vessels, and their number, and their arrangement; and of the pulsating veins and the quiet ones, and the places of their effusion.
And for this reason, Hippocrates said that in a name there is a thought, but more so in the work of small things, and most especially in the art of one's hand.
It is already said of that in the introduction of this book; for whoever does not know what we have told about anatomy will not be able to avoid falling into error by which men are killed, as I have seen many such, because some are formed in this science and leave it for lack of science and experience.
Because you saw a foolish doctor who, in an attempt to lance an abscess of the scrotum on a woman, cut into some arteries of the penis and so much blood flowed that the woman died between her own daughters.
And I saw another doctor who prescribed a steel spike for a man who was in his estate and the spike was great, and he came to him and took out that spike with a piece of the intestines, whereby that man died on the third day.
And I have already cried out for help to remove it, and I saw that because of the size of the spike and the disposition of the sick man's innards, he would not survive.
And I saw another doctor who was taking stipends from a duke of our land for surgery, and he undertook to cure a blacksmith whose arm was fractured in the shoulder blade; and because of his ignorance, he drew out the fracture onto the wound, with splints and staves with great tension, and did not allow the wound any breath.
And he left the man in such condition that it was as if they had not intervened at all, and the arm remained swollen, and an abscess formed between the shoulder blade and the arm, and it became mortified.
I am summoned to him, and I begin to untie the ligaments, and once this is done, he achieves calmness and an increase in his pain relief.
But meanwhile the corruption was already confirmed in the member, so I couldn't stop it, and the corruption continued to spread through the member before the patient could be helped.
And I saw another physician who pierced an abscess with cancer, and it became ulcerated about three days later, in which time the illness of the lord had been magnified.
Which cause is that the cancerous sore is full of melancholy humour, so it's not that the patient should be pierced with instruments all over, but rather the member which suffers should be entirely cauterized.
And for that reason, sons, it is necessary for you to divide the operation into two divisions: one in which salvation is associated with the operation, and in work in which danger lurks in major preparations.
And I have already seen this exist in every place of this book where I mentioned an operation in which there was error and fear; so it is necessary for you to guard against this and leave that out, lest the pages become confused and mislead you into speaking and acting dishonestly.
And therefore, take up your arms with solicitude and protection of God, and your patients with felicity and firmness.
Use the best remedy through God's grace to heal and achieve salvation and glorious success, and overcome terrible diseases with difficult treatment, and hold back your instruments of that which causes you fear, so that the situation may remain in your faith and in your name does not escape from you: because it remains more for your glory, and even more so in the world and at last to your sanctity.

The species of fractures are numerous, and their figures are diverse, and the causes are distinct, because some fractures are caused by a blow from an axe, which breaks through all the bone to the skull, which is left beneath the bone, just as it would be if it were in the trunk of a tree, and this type is called axial; or the axe penetrates some part of the bone, and penetrates the flesh of him, but does not pass the incision into his back; and this species of fracture is called absolute elevation.
And the wound of these two fractures is either great or small, and of compression or crushing; and its cause is a blow with a hammer or club on a rock, and similar things.
And this fracture is either penetrating, which comes close to the periosteum, which is beneath the bone; or it's on the surface of the bone, and the wound of these two fractures is either great or small.
And this type of fracture is one that is split in the bone, in the fineness of a small hair, and therefore it's called this species "capillary fracture". (Note: I've kept the original terminology, which might seem unusual to modern readers)

And this kind of fracture is most often made in the soft bones of humans, such as the skulls of infants.
